<p><b>Shinzō Abe</b> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">安倍 晋三</span><span class="t_nihongo_comma" style="display:none">,</span> <i>Abe Shinzō</i><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>, <small>IPA: </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Japanese" title="Help:IPA for Japanese">[abe ɕiɴzoː]</a></span>; born 21 September 1954)</span> is the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Japan" title="Prime Minister of Japan">Prime Minister of Japan</a> and the President of the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democratic_Party_(Japan)" title="Liberal Democratic Party (Japan)">Liberal Democratic Party</a> (LDP). He is <a href="/wiki/List_of_Prime_Ministers_of_Japan#Rank_by_tenure" title="List of Prime Ministers of Japan">one of the longest serving</a> PMs in Japanese history.</p>
<p>Hailing from a politically prominent family, at age 52, Abe became Japan's youngest post-war prime minister, and the first to be born after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, when he was elected by a special session of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diet_of_Japan" title="Diet of Japan">National Diet</a> in September 2006. Abe resigned on 12 September 2007, for health reasons. Abe was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Yasuo_Fukuda" title="Yasuo Fukuda">Yasuo Fukuda</a>, beginning a string of five Prime Ministers, none of whom retained office for more than sixteen months, before Abe staged a political comeback.</p>
<p>On 26 September 2012, Abe defeated former <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Defense_(Japan)" title="Minister of Defense (Japan)">Minister of Defense</a> <a href="/wiki/Shigeru_Ishiba" title="Shigeru Ishiba">Shigeru Ishiba</a> for the LDP presidency. Following the LDP's landslide victory in the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_general_election,_2012" title="Japanese general election, 2012">2012 general election</a>, Abe became the Prime Minister again. Abe is the first former Prime Minister to return to the office since <a href="/wiki/Shigeru_Yoshida" title="Shigeru Yoshida">Shigeru Yoshida</a> in 1948. Abe was re-elected at the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_general_election,_2014" title="Japanese general election, 2014">2014 general election</a>, retaining his <a href="/wiki/Supermajority" title="Supermajority">two-thirds majority</a> with coalition partner <a href="/wiki/Komeito" title="Komeito">Komeito</a>.</p>
<p>On the 70th anniversary of Japan's defeat in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Abe's cabinet decided to follow previous governments' position on Japan's wartime past by using words such as "apology," "colonization," "aggression," and "remorseful regret" to explain Japan's reaction to the crimes it perpetrated during the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Second_term_as_Prime_Minister_.282012.E2.80.932014.29"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Second term as Prime Minister (2012–2014)</span></a>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Abe was born in <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a> to a politically prominent family. His family is originally from <a href="/wiki/Yamaguchi_Prefecture" title="Yamaguchi Prefecture">Yamaguchi Prefecture</a>, and Abe's registered residence ("honseki chi") is <a href="/wiki/Nagato,_Yamaguchi" title="Nagato, Yamaguchi">Nagato, Yamaguchi</a>, where his grandfather was born. His grandfather, <a href="/wiki/Kan_Abe" title="Kan Abe">Kan Abe</a>, and father, <a href="/wiki/Shintaro_Abe" title="Shintaro Abe">Shintaro Abe</a>, were both politicians. Abe's mother, Yoko Kishi,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> is the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Nobusuke_Kishi" title="Nobusuke Kishi">Nobusuke Kishi</a>, prime minister of Japan from 1957 to 1960. Kishi had been a member of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hideki_T%C5%8Dj%C5%8D" title="Hideki Tōjō">Tōjō</a> Cabinet during the Second World War. Since <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Commander_for_the_Allied_Powers" title="Supreme Commander for the Allied Powers">GHQ's</a> policy changed and became more <a href="/wiki/Anti-communism" title="Anti-communism">anti-communist</a>, Kishi was released from <a href="/wiki/Sugamo_Prison" title="Sugamo Prison">Sugamo Prison</a>, and later established the Japan Democratic Party. In his book "<i>Utsukushii Kuni e</i>" ("Toward a Beautiful Country"), Abe wrote "Some people used to point to my grandfather as a 'Class-A war criminal suspect', and I felt strong repulsion. Because of that experience, I may have become emotionally attached to 'conservatism', on the contrary".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1955, <a href="/wiki/Shigeru_Yoshida" title="Shigeru Yoshida">Shigeru Yoshida</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(1945)" title="Liberal Party (1945)">Liberal Party</a> and Kishi's Democratic Party merged as an anti-leftist coalition and was reestablished as the LDP. Abe attended Seikei Elementary School, Seikei Junior High School and Seikei Senior High School.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> He studied <a href="/wiki/Public_administration" title="Public administration">public administration</a> and graduated with the <a href="/wiki/Bachelor%27s_degree" title="Bachelor's degree">Bachelor's degree</a> in <a href="/wiki/Political_science" title="Political science">Political Science</a> from <a href="/wiki/Seikei_University" title="Seikei University">Seikei University</a> in 1977. He later moved to the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and studied <a href="/wiki/Public_policy" title="Public policy">public policy</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Southern_California" title="University of Southern California">University of Southern California</a>'s <a href="/wiki/USC_Sol_Price_School_of_Public_Policy" title="USC Sol Price School of Public Policy">School of Public Policy</a> for three semesters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USC_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-USC-5">[5]</a></sup> In April 1979, Abe began working for <a href="/wiki/Kobe_Steel" title="Kobe Steel">Kobe Steel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PROFILE_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-PROFILE-6">[6]</a></sup> He left the company in 1982 and pursued a number of government positions including executive assistant to the <a href="/wiki/Minister_for_Foreign_Affairs_(Japan)" title="Minister for Foreign Affairs (Japan)">Minister for Foreign Affairs</a>, private secretary to the chairperson of the LDP General Council, and private secretary to the LDP secretary-general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CHAIR_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-CHAIR-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Member_of_the_House_of_Representatives_.281993.E2.80.932006.29">Member of the House of Representatives (1993–2006)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Member of the House of Representatives (1993–2006)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Robert_Zoellick_meets_Shinzo_Abe_2006-01-23.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="479" data-file-width="600" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Robert_Zoellick_meets_Shinzo_Abe_2006-01-23.jpg/220px-Robert_Zoellick_meets_Shinzo_Abe_2006-01-23.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Robert_Zoellick_meets_Shinzo_Abe_2006-01-23.jpg/330px-Robert_Zoellick_meets_Shinzo_Abe_2006-01-23.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Robert_Zoellick_meets_Shinzo_Abe_2006-01-23.jpg/440px-Robert_Zoellick_meets_Shinzo_Abe_2006-01-23.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Robert_Zoellick_meets_Shinzo_Abe_2006-01-23.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Shinzō Abe (right), as Chief Cabinet Secretary, meets with U.S. Deputy Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Robert_Zoellick" title="Robert Zoellick">Robert Zoellick</a> in January 2006.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shinzō Abe was elected to the first district of <a href="/wiki/Yamaguchi_Prefecture" title="Yamaguchi Prefecture">Yamaguchi Prefecture</a> in 1993 after his father's death in 1991, winning the most votes of the four Representatives elected in the <a href="/wiki/Single_non-transferable_vote" title="Single non-transferable vote">SNTV</a> multi-member district. In 1999, he became Director of the Social Affairs Division, Deputy Chief Cabinet Secretary in the <a href="/wiki/Yoshir%C5%8D_Mori" title="Yoshirō Mori">Yoshirō Mori</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Junichir%C5%8D_Koizumi" title="Junichirō Koizumi">Junichirō Koizumi</a> Cabinets from 2000–2003, after which he was appointed Secretary General of the Liberal Democratic Party.</p>
<p>Abe is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democratic_Party_(Japan)#Seiwa_Seisaku_Kenkyukai_.28Mori_Faction.29" title="Liberal Democratic Party (Japan)">Mori Faction</a> (formally, the Seiwa Seisaku Kenkyū-kai) of the Liberal Democratic Party. This faction is headed by former prime minister <a href="/wiki/Yoshir%C5%8D_Mori" title="Yoshirō Mori">Yoshirō Mori</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jun%27ichir%C5%8D_Koizumi" title="Jun'ichirō Koizumi">Jun'ichirō Koizumi</a> was a member of the Mori Faction prior to leaving it, as is the custom when accepting a high party post. From 1986 to 1991, Abe's father, Shintaro, headed the same faction. The Seiwa Seisaku Kenkyū-kai has 60 members in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/House_of_Representatives_of_Japan" title="House of Representatives of Japan">House of Representatives</a> and 26 in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/House_of_Councillors" title="House of Councillors">House of Councillors</a>.</p>
<p>In 2000, Abe's home and the office of his supporters in <a href="/wiki/Shimonoseki" title="Shimonoseki">Shimonoseki</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Yamaguchi_Prefecture" title="Yamaguchi Prefecture">Yamaguchi Prefecture</a>, were attacked with <a href="/wiki/Molotov_cocktail" title="Molotov cocktail">molotov cocktails</a> on numerous occasions. The perpetrators were several <a href="/wiki/Yakuza" title="Yakuza">yakuza</a> members belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Kudo-kai" title="Kudo-kai">Kudo-kai</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Kitakyushu" title="Kitakyushu">Kitakyushu</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Yakuza#Designated_boryokudan" title="Yakuza">designated boryokudan</a> syndicate. The reason for the attacks was believed to be that Abe's local aide refused to give cash to a Shimonoseki real estate broker in return for supporting a Shimonoseki mayoral candidate in 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Abe was chief <a href="/wiki/Negotiation" title="Negotiation">negotiator</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Japan" title="Government of Japan">Japanese government</a> on behalf of the families of Japanese <a href="/wiki/Kidnapping" title="Kidnapping">abductees</a> taken to <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>. As a part of the effort, he accompanied Koizumi to meet <a href="/wiki/Kim_Jong-il" title="Kim Jong-il">Kim Jong‑il</a> in 2002. He gained national popularity when he demanded that Japanese abductees visiting Japan remain, in defiance of North Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DEFIANCE_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-DEFIANCE-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>He was the leader of a project team within the LDP that did a survey on "excessive sexual education and gender-free education". Among the items to which this team raised objections were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomical_doll" title="Anatomical doll">anatomical dolls</a> and other curricular materials "not taking into consideration the age of children", school policies banning traditional boys' and girls' festivals, and mixed-gender <a href="/wiki/Physical_education" title="Physical education">physical education</a>. The team sought to provide contrast to the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_of_Japan" title="Democratic Party of Japan">Democratic Party of Japan</a>, which it alleged supported such policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEXEDU_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-SEXEDU-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>On September 20, 2006, Abe was elected as the president of the ruling Liberal Democratic Party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ELECTED_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-ELECTED-11">[11]</a></sup> His chief competitors for the position were <a href="/wiki/Sadakazu_Tanigaki" title="Sadakazu Tanigaki">Sadakazu Tanigaki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tar%C5%8D_As%C5%8D" title="Tarō Asō">Tarō Asō</a>. <a href="/wiki/Yasuo_Fukuda" title="Yasuo Fukuda">Yasuo Fukuda</a> was a leading early contender but ultimately chose not to run. Former Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Yoshir%C5%8D_Mori" title="Yoshirō Mori">Yoshirō Mori</a>, to whose faction both Abe and Fukuda belonged, stated that the faction strongly leant toward Abe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FACTION_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FACTION-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="First_term_as_Prime_Minister_.282006.E2.80.932007.29">First term as Prime Minister (2006–2007)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: First term as Prime Minister (2006–2007)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ambox_current_red.svg"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="360" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section's <b><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute">factual accuracy</a> may be compromised due to out-of-date information</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <small><i>(November 2012)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>On 26 September 2006, Abe was elected prime minister with 339 of 475 votes in the Diet's lower house and a firm majority in the upper house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-13">[13]</a></sup> Abe, elected at age 52, was the youngest prime minister since <a href="/wiki/Fumimaro_Konoe" title="Fumimaro Konoe">Fumimaro Konoe</a> in 1941.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-age_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-age-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_policy">Domestic policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Domestic policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Abe expressed a general commitment to the fiscal reforms instituted by his predecessor, Jun'ichirō Koizumi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-age_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-age-14">[14]</a></sup> He has taken some steps toward balancing the Japanese budget, such as appointing a tax policy expert, <a href="/wiki/K%C5%8Dji_Omi" title="Kōji Omi">Kōji Omi</a>, as Minister of Finance. Omi has previously supported increases in the national <a href="/wiki/Consumption_tax" title="Consumption tax">consumption tax</a>, although Abe has distanced himself from this policy and seeks to achieve much of his budget balancing through spending cuts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-omiarticle_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-omiarticle-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Since 1997, as the bureau chief of "Institute of Junior Assembly Members Who Think About The Outlook of Japan and History Education", Abe supported the controversial <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Society_for_History_Textbook_Reform" title="Japanese Society for History Textbook Reform">Japanese Society for History Textbook Reform</a> and the <i>New History Textbook</i>.</p>
<p>In March 2007, Abe along with right-wing politicians have proposed a bill to encourage nationalism and a "love for one's country and hometown" among the Japanese youth (specific wording from the revised "fundamental law of education" <span lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">教育基本法</span>, which was revised to include "love of country" despite much criticism).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (June 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Imperial_household">Imperial household</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Imperial household">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Abe held conservative views in the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_succession_controversy" title="Japanese succession controversy">Japanese succession controversy</a>, and shortly after the birth of <a href="/wiki/Prince_Hisahito_of_Akishino" title="Prince Hisahito of Akishino">Prince Hisahito of Akishino</a> he abandoned a proposed legislative amendment to permit women to inherit the <a href="/wiki/Chrysanthemum_Throne" title="Chrysanthemum Throne">Chrysanthemum Throne</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy">Foreign policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Foreign policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:254px;max-width:254px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:33rdG8Leaders.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="322" data-file-width="514" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/33rdG8Leaders.jpg/250px-33rdG8Leaders.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/33rdG8Leaders.jpg/375px-33rdG8Leaders.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/33rdG8Leaders.jpg/500px-33rdG8Leaders.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/33rd_G8_summit" title="33rd G8 summit">33rd G8 summit</a> at <a href="/wiki/Heiligendamm" title="Heiligendamm">Heiligendamm</a> in June 2007</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bush_Abe,_Camp_David.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="388" data-file-width="514" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Bush_Abe%2C_Camp_David.jpg/250px-Bush_Abe%2C_Camp_David.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Bush_Abe%2C_Camp_David.jpg/375px-Bush_Abe%2C_Camp_David.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Bush_Abe%2C_Camp_David.jpg/500px-Bush_Abe%2C_Camp_David.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Abe shakes hands with then U.S. President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> in April 2007</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="North_Korea">North Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: North Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Narendra_Modi_and_Shinzo_Abe,_2014.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="558" data-file-width="640" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Narendra_Modi_and_Shinzo_Abe%2C_2014.jpg/220px-Narendra_Modi_and_Shinzo_Abe%2C_2014.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Narendra_Modi_and_Shinzo_Abe%2C_2014.jpg/330px-Narendra_Modi_and_Shinzo_Abe%2C_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Narendra_Modi_and_Shinzo_Abe%2C_2014.jpg/440px-Narendra_Modi_and_Shinzo_Abe%2C_2014.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Narendra_Modi_and_Shinzo_Abe,_2014.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Abe with current <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_India" title="Prime Minister of India">Prime Minister of India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Narendra_Modi" title="Narendra Modi">Narendra Modi</a>, in 2014</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shinzō Abe has generally taken a hard-line stance with respect to <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>, especially regarding the <a href="/wiki/North_Korean_abductions_of_Japanese_citizens" title="North Korean abductions of Japanese citizens">North Korean abductions of Japanese citizens</a>.</p>
<p>In 2002 negotiations between Japan and North Korea, Prime Minister Koizumi and General Secretary <a href="/wiki/Kim_Jong-il" title="Kim Jong-il">Kim Jong-il</a> agreed to give abductees permission to visit Japan. A few weeks into the visit, the Japanese government decided that the abductees would be restricted from returning to North Korea where their families live. Abe took credit for this policy decision in his best-selling book, <i>Towards a Beautiful Nation</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">美しい国へ</span><span class="t_nihongo_comma" style="display:none">,</span> <i>Utsukushii kuni e</i><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>)</span>. North Korea criticized this Japanese decision as a breach of a diplomatic promise, and the negotiations aborted.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="China.2C_South_Korea.2C_and_Taiwan">China, South Korea, and Taiwan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: China, South Korea, and Taiwan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Abe has publicly recognized the need for improved relations with the <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">People's Republic of China</a> and, along with Foreign Minister Taro Aso, sought an eventual summit meeting with former Chinese paramount leader <a href="/wiki/Hu_Jintao" title="Hu Jintao">Hu Jintao</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNN_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNN-17">[17]</a></sup> Abe has also said that <a href="/wiki/China%E2%80%93Japan_relations" title="China–Japan relations">China–Japan relations</a> should not continue to be based on emotions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMOTION_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-EMOTION-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Occasionally, Abe is respected among politicians in <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> who are part of the <a href="/wiki/Pan-Green_Coalition" title="Pan-Green Coalition">Pan-Green Coalition</a> seeking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taiwan_independence" title="Taiwan independence">Taiwanese independence</a>. <a href="/wiki/Chen_Shui-bian" title="Chen Shui-bian">Chen Shui-bian</a> welcomed Abe's ministership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mainichi_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mainichi-19">[19]</a></sup> Part of Abe's appeal in Taiwan is historical: his grandfather Nobusuke Kishi was pro-Taiwan, and his great-uncle <a href="/wiki/Eisaku_Sat%C5%8D" title="Eisaku Satō">Eisaku Satō</a> was the last prime minister to visit Taiwan while in office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mainichi_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mainichi-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Abe has expressed the need to strengthen political, security, and economic ties within the Southeast Asian region. Abe has increased its allies in its international campaign to counter the North Korean nuclear cards. So far, Abe has successfully visited the Philippines and Indonesia, and although China is not within the Southeast Asian region, Japan has also sought for their support. However, relations with China continue to be tarnished by the <a href="/wiki/Senkaku_Islands_dispute" title="Senkaku Islands dispute">Senkaku Islands dispute</a> and Abe's visits to Yasukuni shrine (see below).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Abe, in his two terms as the prime minister of Japan, sought to upgrade the strategic Japan-India relationship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Abe initiated the <a href="/wiki/Quadrilateral_Security_Dialogue" title="Quadrilateral Security Dialogue">Quadrilateral Security Dialogue</a> between Japan, the United States, Australia and India in 2007. His three-day visit to <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> in August 2007 inaugurated a new bilateral Asian alliance, building on the long history of <a href="/wiki/India%E2%80%93Japan_relations" title="India–Japan relations">strong, friendly bilateral relations</a> between India and Japan. Abe's initiative is to establish the "fifth" bilateral link in an emerging scenario, whereby, the U.S.–Australia, U.S.–Japan, Japan–Australia, and U.S.–India links are supportive strategic alignments. A sixth link of the India-Australia would be the logical corollary, formalized as a new quadrilateral of strategic bulwark. The eventual expansion to include Vietnam, South Korea, Philippines and Indonesia, in this arrangement, has been speculated in the media of those states. Chinese strategic experts have labelled the evolving geo-strategic paradigm, the "Asian <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Abe's pragmatic India foreign policy, is to boost Japan's resurgent economic indicators, while gaining a crucial partner in Asia. India, unlike most major Far-Eastern and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ASEAN" title="ASEAN">ASEAN</a> states, does not have a history of serious military dispute with Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defense">Defense</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Defense">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Abe also sought to revise or broaden the interpretation of <a href="/wiki/Article_9_of_the_Japanese_Constitution" title="Article 9 of the Japanese Constitution">Article 9 of the Japanese Constitution</a> in order to permit Japan to maintain <a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">de jure</a> military forces. He had stated that "we are reaching the limit in narrowing down differences between Japan's security and the interpretation of our constitution".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newshour_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-newshour-23">[23]</a></sup> During his first period as prime minister he upgraded the Japan Defense Agency to <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Defense_(Japan)" title="Ministry of Defense (Japan)">full ministry status</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Like his predecessors, he supported the Japanese alliance with the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unpopularity_and_sudden_resignation">Unpopularity and sudden resignation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Unpopularity and sudden resignation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democratic_Party_(Japan)_leadership_election,_2007" title="Liberal Democratic Party (Japan) leadership election, 2007">Liberal Democratic Party (Japan) leadership election, 2007</a></div>
<p>Abe's ruling Liberal Democratic Party suffered great losses in the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_House_of_Councillors_election,_2007" title="Japanese House of Councillors election, 2007">upper house election</a>, marking the first time it had lost control in 52 years. Another agricultural minister, <a href="/wiki/Norihiko_Akagi" title="Norihiko Akagi">Norihiko Akagi</a>, who was involved in a political funding scandal, resigned after the election.</p>
<p>In an attempt to revive his administration, Abe announced a new cabinet on 27 August 2007. However, the new agricultural minister <a href="/wiki/Takehiko_Endo" title="Takehiko Endo">Takehiko Endo</a>, involved in a finance scandal, resigned only 7 days later.</p>
<p>On 12 September 2007, only three days after a new parliamentary session had begun, Abe announced his intention to resign his position as prime minister at an unscheduled press conference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Abe said his unpopularity was hindering the passage of an anti-terrorism law, involving among other things Japan's continued military presence in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>. Party officials also said the embattled prime minister was suffering from poor health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> On 26 September 2007 Abe officially ended his term as <a href="/wiki/Yasuo_Fukuda" title="Yasuo Fukuda">Yasuo Fukuda</a> became the new prime minister of Japan.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Inter-premiership_.282007.E2.80.932012.29">Inter-premiership (2007–2012)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Inter-premiership (2007–2012)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Abe later revealed that the illness that contributed to ending his first term as Prime Minister was <a href="/wiki/Ulcerative_colitis" title="Ulcerative colitis">ulcerative colitis</a>, but that he has since recovered due to access to a drug, <a href="/wiki/Mesalazine" title="Mesalazine">Asacol</a>, that was previously unavailable in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> When he returned to office he used his own case to argue for lessening the time it takes to approve potentially innovative drugs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> After resigning as Prime Minister, Abe remained in the National Diet and was re-elected in his <a href="/wiki/Yamaguchi_4th_district" title="Yamaguchi 4th district">Yamaguchi 4th district</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_general_election,_2009" title="Japanese general election, 2009">2009 election</a> when the LDP lost power to the Democratic Party of Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Presidential_election_speech_of_the_Liberal_Democratic_Party_in_Nagano.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2474" data-file-width="4841" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Presidential_election_speech_of_the_Liberal_Democratic_Party_in_Nagano.jpg/220px-Presidential_election_speech_of_the_Liberal_Democratic_Party_in_Nagano.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Presidential_election_speech_of_the_Liberal_Democratic_Party_in_Nagano.jpg/330px-Presidential_election_speech_of_the_Liberal_Democratic_Party_in_Nagano.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Presidential_election_speech_of_the_Liberal_Democratic_Party_in_Nagano.jpg/440px-Presidential_election_speech_of_the_Liberal_Democratic_Party_in_Nagano.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Presidential_election_speech_of_the_Liberal_Democratic_Party_in_Nagano.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Abe and other candidates campaigning during the LDP presidential election in 2012. His chief rival, Shigeru Ishiba, is standing immediately to his right</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 26 September 2012, Abe was re-elected as president of the opposition Liberal Democratic Party defeating former Defense Minister Shigeru Ishiba in a runoff vote by 108 votes to 89.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Abe returned to the LDP leadership at a time of political turmoil, the governing DPJ had lost its majority in the lower house due to party splits over nuclear policies and the cabinet's move to raise the consumption tax from 5 to 10 percent. Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Yoshihiko_Noda" title="Yoshihiko Noda">Yoshihiko Noda</a> was forced to rely on the LDP to pass the Consumption Tax bill and in return was pressured by Abe and the opposition parties to hold a snap general election. Noda agreed to this on the conditions that the LDP passed a bond-financing bill, and would support a commission to reform the social security system and address electoral malapportionment in the next diet session.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2012_general_election">2012 general election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 2012 general election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 16 November 2012, Prime Minister Noda announced the dissolution of the lower house and that the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_general_election,_2012" title="Japanese general election, 2012">general election</a> would be held on 16 December.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Abe campaigned using the slogan <i>"Nippon o Torimodosu"</i> (<i>"Take back Japan"</i>), promising economic revival through monetary easing, higher public spending and the continued use of nuclear energy, and a tough line in territorial disputes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the elections on 16 December 2012, the LDP won 294 seats in the 480 seat House of Representatives. Together with the New Komeito Party (which has partnered with the LDP since the late 1990s), Abe was able to form a coalition government that controlled a two thirds majority in the lower house, allowing it to override the upper house's veto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_term_as_Prime_Minister_.282012.E2.80.932014.29">Second term as Prime Minister (2012–2014)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Second term as Prime Minister (2012–2014)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On 26 December 2012, Abe was formally elected as Prime Minister by the Diet, with the support of 328 out of 480 members of the House of Representatives, he and his second cabinet, which he called a "crisis-busting cabinet", were sworn in later that day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shinoda_2013_230_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shinoda_2013_230-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The new government included LDP heavyweights such as former Prime Minister Tarō Asō as <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Prime_Minister_of_Japan" title="Deputy Prime Minister of Japan">Deputy Prime Minister</a> and Finance Minister, <a href="/wiki/Yoshihide_Suga" title="Yoshihide Suga">Yoshihide Suga</a> as <a href="/wiki/Chief_Cabinet_Secretary" title="Chief Cabinet Secretary">Chief Cabinet Secretary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Akira_Amari" title="Akira Amari">Akira Amari</a> as Economy Minister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shinoda_2013_230_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shinoda_2013_230-37">[37]</a></sup> Following his victory, Abe said, "With the strength of my entire cabinet, I will implement bold monetary policy, flexible fiscal policy and a growth strategy that encourages private investment, and with these three policy pillars, achieve results."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shinzo_Abe_at_CSIS.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="1024" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Shinzo_Abe_at_CSIS.jpg/220px-Shinzo_Abe_at_CSIS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Shinzo_Abe_at_CSIS.jpg/330px-Shinzo_Abe_at_CSIS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Shinzo_Abe_at_CSIS.jpg/440px-Shinzo_Abe_at_CSIS.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shinzo_Abe_at_CSIS.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Prime Minister Shinzo Abe speaking at <a href="/wiki/Centre_for_Strategic_and_International_Studies" title="Centre for Strategic and International Studies">CSIS</a> in Washington DC, in February 2013</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In February 2013 Abe gave an address at the <a href="/wiki/Centre_for_Strategic_and_International_Studies" title="Centre for Strategic and International Studies">Centre for Strategic and International Studies</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_DC" title="Washington DC">Washington DC</a> in which he explained his economic and diplomatic objectives, and that he had returned to the Prime Ministership to prevent Japan becoming a "Tier Two Nation", declaring that "Japan is back".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abe.2C_Shinzo_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abe.2C_Shinzo-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_policy_.28Abenomics.29">Economic policy (Abenomics)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Economic policy (Abenomics)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Second Abe cabinet revived the Council on Economic and Fiscal Policy (CEFP) that had played a key role in formulating economic policy during the Koizumi cabinet, but had been abandoned by the 2009-12 DPJ administrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Wall_Street_Journal_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Wall_Street_Journal-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Abe declared in his January 2013 policy speech to the Diet that economic revival and escaping deflation was "the greatest and urgent issue" facing Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-japan.kantei.go.jp_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-japan.kantei.go.jp-42">[42]</a></sup> His economic strategy, referred to as <a href="/wiki/Abenomics" title="Abenomics">Abenomics</a>, consists of the so-called "three arrows" (an allusion to <a href="/wiki/M%C5%8Dri_Motonari#Expanding_territory" title="Mōri Motonari">an old Japanese story</a>) of policy. The first arrow is monetary expansion aimed at achieving a 2% inflation target, the second a flexible fiscal policy to act as an economic stimulus in the short term, then achieve a budget surplus and the third, a growth strategy focusing on structural reform and private sector investment to achieve long-term growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abe.2C_Shinzo_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Abe.2C_Shinzo-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id=".22First_Arrow.22:_Monetary_policy">"First Arrow": Monetary policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title='Edit section: "First Arrow": Monetary policy'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Haruhiko_Kuroda_at_ADB_Philippines_(crop).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1780" data-file-width="1590" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Haruhiko_Kuroda_at_ADB_Philippines_%28crop%29.jpg/170px-Haruhiko_Kuroda_at_ADB_Philippines_%28crop%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Haruhiko_Kuroda_at_ADB_Philippines_%28crop%29.jpg/255px-Haruhiko_Kuroda_at_ADB_Philippines_%28crop%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Haruhiko_Kuroda_at_ADB_Philippines_%28crop%29.jpg/340px-Haruhiko_Kuroda_at_ADB_Philippines_%28crop%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Haruhiko_Kuroda_at_ADB_Philippines_(crop).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Haruhiko_Kuroda" title="Haruhiko Kuroda">Haruhiko Kuroda</a>, whom Abe appointed as Governor of the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Japan" title="Bank of Japan">Bank of Japan</a> in Spring 2013, has implemented the "first arrow" monetary policy</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the first meeting of the CEFP on 9 January 2013 Abe declared that the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Japan" title="Bank of Japan">Bank of Japan</a> should follow a policy of monetary easing with a view to achieving a target of 2 percent inflation. Abe maintained pressure on the Bank's governor, <a href="/wiki/Masaaki_Shirakawa" title="Masaaki Shirakawa">Masaaki Shirakawa</a>, who was reluctant to set specific targets, into agreeing to the policy. In February, after Abe publicly speculated that the government could legislate to strip the bank of independence, Shirakawa announced he was leaving office prematurely before his term expired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Abe then appointed <a href="/wiki/Haruhiko_Kuroda" title="Haruhiko Kuroda">Haruhiko Kuroda</a> as governor, who had previously advocated inflation targets, and who has pursued the government's policies of monetary easing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the first meeting of the Bank's monetary policy committee after he had taken office in April, Kuroda announced an aggressive programme of easing intended to double the money supply and achieve the 2 percent inflation target at "the earliest possible time".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Over the first six months of the second Abe Cabinet, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yen" title="Yen">Yen</a> fell from a high of ¥77 to the dollar to ¥101.8, and the <a href="/wiki/Nikkei_225" title="Nikkei 225">Nikkei 225</a> rose by 70 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a surprise move in October 2014, Kuroda announced that the BOJ would boost the monetary easing programme and accelerate asset purchases, the monetary policy committee split by 5 votes to 4 but supported the policy. This was interpreted as a response to disappointing economic figures in the aftermath of the increase in the consumption tax to 8 percent, inflation having fallen to 1 percent from its peak of 1.5 percent in April.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id=".22Second_Arrow.22:_Fiscal_policy">"Second Arrow": Fiscal policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title='Edit section: "Second Arrow": Fiscal policy'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taro_Aso_in_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_in_Davos_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1500" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Taro_Aso_in_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_in_Davos_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Taro_Aso_in_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_in_Davos_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Taro_Aso_in_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_in_Davos_%28cropped%29.jpg/255px-Taro_Aso_in_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_in_Davos_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Taro_Aso_in_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_in_Davos_%28cropped%29.jpg/340px-Taro_Aso_in_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_in_Davos_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Taro_Aso_in_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_in_Davos_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Abe's Minister of Finance <a href="/wiki/Tar%C5%8D_As%C5%8D" title="Tarō Asō">Tarō Asō</a>, who also serves as Deputy Prime Minister</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Abe Cabinet's first budget included a 10.3 trillion yen stimulus package, composed of public works spending, aid for small businesses and investment incentives, that aimed to increase growth by 2 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The budget also increased defense spending and manpower while reducing foreign aid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Autumn 2013 Abe made the decision to proceed with the first stage of the increase in the consumption tax from 5 to 8 percent in April 2014 (with a second stage envisaged raising it to 10 percent in October 2015). The bill to raise the tax had been passed under the previous DPJ government, but the final decision lay with the Prime Minister. He and Finance Minister Taro Aso explained that the tax would be increased to provide a "sustainable" basis for future social spending, and to avoid the need to finance future stimulus by issuing government bonds. While this was expected to affect economic growth in the quarter following the rise, Abe also announced a 5 trillion yen stimulus package that aimed to mitigate any effects on economic revival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> After the increase in April, Japan fell into recession during the second and third quarters of 2014, leading to Abe delaying the second stage of the tax rise until April 2017 and calling a <a href="/wiki/Japanese_general_election,_2014" title="Japanese general election, 2014">snap election</a> (see below).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> In response to the recession, Aso announced that the government would ask the Diet to pass a supplementary budget to fund a further stimulus package worth 2–3 trillion yen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>There has been some division within the Abe cabinet between "fiscal hawks", such as Finance Minister Aso, who favour fiscal consolidation through spending cuts and tax increases, and reflationists, such as Abe himself, who argue in favour of a "growth first" policy that prioritises economic expansion and recovery over budget considerations using the slogan "no fiscal health without economic revitalization".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abe.E2.80.99s_Growth-First_Fiscal_Policy_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abe.E2.80.99s_Growth-First_Fiscal_Policy-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Abe's decision to delay the consumption tax increase in November 2014 and his push for a large fiscal deficit in the 2015 budget without social security cuts was interpreted as a victory for this faction within the LDP. The government did however, commit to a primary surplus by 2020, and pledged to review its strategy in 2018 if the primary deficit had not fallen to 1 percent of GDP by that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abe.E2.80.99s_Growth-First_Fiscal_Policy_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Abe.E2.80.99s_Growth-First_Fiscal_Policy-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id=".22Third_Arrow.22:_Growth_strategy_and_structural_reform">"Third Arrow": Growth strategy and structural reform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title='Edit section: "Third Arrow": Growth strategy and structural reform'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On 15 March 2013 Abe announced that Japan was entering negotiations to join the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Pacific_Partnership" title="Trans-Pacific Partnership">Trans-Pacific Partnership</a>, this was interpreted by analysts as a means through which the government can enact reforms to liberalise certain sectors of the Japanese economy, most notably agriculture, and was criticised by farm lobbies and some sections of the LDP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Economist Yoshizaki Tatsuhiko described the TPP as having the potential to act as the "linchpin of Abe’s economic revitalization strategy" by making Japan more competitive through free trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> In February 2015 the Abe government struck a deal to limit the power of the <a href="/wiki/Central_Union_of_Agricultural_Cooperatives" title="Central Union of Agricultural Cooperatives">JA-Zenchu</a> body to supervise and audit Japan's agricultural co-operatives, in a move designed to facilitate TPP negotiations, improve the competitiveness of Japan's farming sector and curtail the influence of the agriculture lobby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Abe revealed the first measures related to the "third arrow" policies in June 2013, which included plans to establish <a href="/wiki/Special_economic_zone" title="Special economic zone">deregulated economic zones</a> and allow the sale of drugs online, but did not include substantial measures related to labour market or business reform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> These measures were less well received than the first two arrows had been since Abe took office, with the stock market falling slightly and critics arguing that they lacked detail, <a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a>, for example judged the announcement a "misfire".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Analysts did note, however, that Abe was waiting until after the July Upper House elections to reveal further details, to avoid an adverse reaction by voters to potentially unpopular reforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> At the annual meeting of the <a href="/wiki/World_Economic_Forum" title="World Economic Forum">World Economic Forum</a> in Davos in 2014 Abe announced that he was ready to act as a "drill bit" to break through the rock of vested interests and "red tape" to achieve structural reforms of the economy. He cited reforms of agriculture, energy and health sectors as evidence of this, and pledged to push forward with the TPP, a Japan-EU trade deal and tax, corporate governance and planning reforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Abe announced a package of structural reforms in June 2014, that the Economist described as "less a single arrow than a 1,000-strong bundle" and compared favourably to the 2013 announcement. These new measures included corporate governance reform, the easing of restrictions on hiring foreign staff in special economic zones, liberalising the health sector and measures to help foreign and local entrepreneurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The plans also included a cut in corporation tax to below 30 percent, an expansion of childcare to encourage women to join the workforce, and the loosening of restrictions on overtime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> In December 2015, the government announced corporation tax would be reduced to 29.97 percent in 2016, bringing the cut forward one year ahead of schedule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amari_Akira_1-3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="352" data-file-width="264" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Amari_Akira_1-3.jpg/170px-Amari_Akira_1-3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Amari_Akira_1-3.jpg/255px-Amari_Akira_1-3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9d/Amari_Akira_1-3.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amari_Akira_1-3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Akira_Amari" title="Akira Amari">Akira Amari</a>, who served as Abe's economy minister from 2012 to 2016, oversaw the "third arrow" growth strategy and negotiations to join the Trans-Pacific Partnership agreement.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In September 2013 Abe called for a "society in which all women can shine", setting a target that 30 percent of leadership positions should be held by women by 2020. Abe cited the "womenomics" ideas of <a href="/wiki/Kathy_Matsui" title="Kathy Matsui">Kathy Matsui</a> that greater participation by women in the workforce, which is relatively low in Japan, especially in leadership roles, could improve Japan's GDP and potentially fertility rates, in spite of declining population figures. The Abe cabinet has introduced measures to expand childcare and legislation to force public and private organisations to publish data on the number of women they employ, and what positions they hold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>In November 2013 the Abe cabinet passed a bill to liberalise Japan's electricity market by abolishing price controls, breaking up regional monopolies, separating power transmission from generation by creating a national grid company. This move was partly in response to the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">2011 Fukushima disaster</a>, and the bill faced little opposition in the Diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> By March 2015, more than 500 companies had applied to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Economy,_Trade_and_Industry_(Japan)" title="Ministry of Economy, Trade and Industry (Japan)">Economy Ministry</a> to enter the electricity retail market and electricity industry is expected to be fully liberalised by 2016, with gas utilities following suit by 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Abe has also said he favours the re-building of Japan's nuclear reactors following the Fukushima disaster(though much of the authority to restart nuclear plants lies with local governments) and plans to strengthen relations with the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2013 the Eurekahedge Japan Hedge Fund Index posted a record 28 percent return, which was credited to the unprecedented actions of the Abe government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> In July 2015 the IMF reported that, while the structural reforms had "modestly" improved growth prospects, "further high-impact structural reforms are needed to lift growth" and prevent over-reliance on yen depreciation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2013_Upper_House_election">2013 Upper House election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: 2013 Upper House election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When Abe returned to office, although neither party had controlled the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Councillors_(Japan)" title="House of Councillors (Japan)">House of Councillors</a> (the upper house of the Diet) since the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_House_of_Councillors_election,_2007" title="Japanese House of Councillors election, 2007">2007 election</a>, the opposition DPJ was the largest party. The governing coalition enjoyed a two thirds majority in the lower house that allowed it to override the upper house's veto, but this requires a delay of 90 days. This situation, known as the "Twisted Diet", had contributed to political gridlock and the "revolving door" of Prime Ministers since 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Abe's campaign for the 2013 election focused on themes of economic revival, asking voters to give him a stable mandate in both houses to pursue reforms, and took a more moderate tone on defense and constitutional matters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_House_of_Councillors_election,_2013" title="Japanese House of Councillors election, 2013">July 2013 upper house election</a>, the LDP emerged as the largest party with 115 seats (a gain of 31) and the Komeito with 20 (a gain of 1), giving Abe's coalition control of both houses of the Diet, but not the two thirds majority in the upper house that would allow for constitutional revision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> With no national elections due until 2016, this result was described as giving Abe the opportunity of "three golden years" of parliamentary stability in which to implement his policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_policy_2">Domestic policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Domestic policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_2">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Abe's return to the Prime Ministership saw a renewed attempt to downplay Japan's wartime atrocities in school textbooks, an issue that had contributed to his earlier downfall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> In 2013 Abe supported the creation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Super_Global_Universities" title="Super Global Universities">Super Global Universities</a> program. This is a ten-year program to increase international student attendance in Japanese universities and hire more foreign faculty. There is also funding for selected universities to create English-only undergraduate programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-QS_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-QS-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EAF_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-EAF-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_growth">Population growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Population growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2014 Abe allocated millions of dollars of the fiscal budget to help programs that help single individuals of Japan find potential mates. These programs entitled "Marriage support programs" were started in hopes of raising Japan's declining birthrate which is half of what it was six decades ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy_2">Foreign policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Foreign policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shinzo_Abe_with_Barack_Obama_laughing,_April_2014.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1500" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Shinzo_Abe_with_Barack_Obama_laughing%2C_April_2014.jpg/220px-Shinzo_Abe_with_Barack_Obama_laughing%2C_April_2014.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Shinzo_Abe_with_Barack_Obama_laughing%2C_April_2014.jpg/330px-Shinzo_Abe_with_Barack_Obama_laughing%2C_April_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Shinzo_Abe_with_Barack_Obama_laughing%2C_April_2014.jpg/440px-Shinzo_Abe_with_Barack_Obama_laughing%2C_April_2014.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shinzo_Abe_with_Barack_Obama_laughing,_April_2014.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Prime Minister Abe with Former US President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> in Tokyo in April 2014</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shortly after taking office Abe signalled a "drastic reshaping" of foreign policy, and promised to pursue diplomacy with a global, rather than a regional or bilateral outlook based on "the fundamental values of freedom, democracy, basic human rights, and the rule of law".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-japan.kantei.go.jp_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-japan.kantei.go.jp-42">[42]</a></sup> His choice of <a href="/wiki/Fumio_Kishida" title="Fumio Kishida">Fumio Kishida</a> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foreign_Minister_of_Japan" title="Foreign Minister of Japan">foreign minister</a> was interpreted as a sign that he would pursue a more moderate line compared to his hawkish stance in the run up the general election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Wall_Street_Journal_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Wall_Street_Journal-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Within weeks of returning to power, the Second Abe cabinet faced the <a href="/wiki/In_Amenas_hostage_crisis" title="In Amenas hostage crisis">In Amenas hostage crisis</a> of 2013 in which 10 Japanese citizens were killed. Abe condemned the killings as "absolutely unforgivable" and confirmed that Japan and Britain had co-operated over the incident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Abe believed that this incident demonstrated the need for the creation of a <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Council_(Japan)" title="National Security Council (Japan)">Japanese National Security Council</a> (see below), and convened a panel to consider its creation soon after the crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Abe has been unusually active in the field of foreign affairs for a Japanese Prime Minister, making visits to 49 countries between December 2012 and September 2014, a number that has been described as "unprecedented" (by contrast, his immediate two predecessors <a href="/wiki/Naoto_Kan" title="Naoto Kan">Naoto Kan</a> and Yoshihiko Noda visited a combined total of 18 countries between June 2010 and December 2012).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> This was interpreted as a means to offset poor relations with China and Korea by increasing Japan's profile on the world stage and improving bilateral ties with other countries in the region. South East Asian nations, Australia and India have been significant and frequent destinations for Abe, who visited all 10 <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Southeast_Asian_Nations" title="Association of Southeast Asian Nations">ASEAN</a> countries in his first year in office. The diplomatic tours also functioned as another element of Abenomics by promoting Japan to the international business community and opening up avenues for trade, energy and defence procurement deals (for example, business executives often travel with Abe on these visits).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>In September 2013, Abe intervened to aid <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_bid_for_the_2020_Summer_Olympics" title="Tokyo bid for the 2020 Summer Olympics">Tokyo's bid</a> to host the <a href="/wiki/2020_Summer_Olympics" title="2020 Summer Olympics">2020 Summer Olympic and Paralympic games</a>, giving a speech in English at the <a href="/wiki/125th_IOC_Session" title="125th IOC Session">IOC session in Buenos Aires</a>, in which he extolled the role of sport in Japan and sought to reassure the committee that any ongoing issues with the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Power Plant">Fukushima plant</a> were under control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> After the bid was successful, Abe sought to portray the games as symbolic of his Abenomics economic revitalization programme, saying "I want to make the Olympics a trigger for sweeping away 15 years of deflation and economic decline".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> In 2014 he said that he hoped a "robot olympics" would be held at the same time, to promote the robotics industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PM_Modi_and_PM_Abe_at_the_2014_G-20_summit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="684" data-file-width="684" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/PM_Modi_and_PM_Abe_at_the_2014_G-20_summit.jpg/220px-PM_Modi_and_PM_Abe_at_the_2014_G-20_summit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/PM_Modi_and_PM_Abe_at_the_2014_G-20_summit.jpg/330px-PM_Modi_and_PM_Abe_at_the_2014_G-20_summit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/PM_Modi_and_PM_Abe_at_the_2014_G-20_summit.jpg/440px-PM_Modi_and_PM_Abe_at_the_2014_G-20_summit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PM_Modi_and_PM_Abe_at_the_2014_G-20_summit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Abe meets Indian Prime Minister Narendra Modi at the 2014 G20 summit</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Abe's foreign policy has moved Japan away from its traditional focus on the "big three" bilateral relationships with the United States, China, and South Korea, and has sought to increase Japan's international profile by expanding ties with NATO, the EU, and other organisations beyond the Asia-Pacific region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_ASAN_Forum_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_ASAN_Forum-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> In 2014, Abe and British Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> agreed to establish a "2 + 2 framework" of annual consultations between the British and Japanese foreign and defense ministries, with Abe calling for greater co-operation on issues "from peace of the seas to the security of the skies, space and cyberspace". This followed a similar agreement with French ministers in Tokyo earlier in the year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_ASAN_Forum_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_ASAN_Forum-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Abe concluded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japan-Australia_Economic_Partnership_Agreement" title="Japan-Australia Economic Partnership Agreement">Japan-Australia Economic Partnership Agreement</a> with Australia's <a href="/wiki/Abbott_Government" title="Abbott Government">Abbott Government</a> in 2014, and addressed a joint sitting of the Australian Parliament in July.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> In heralding the agreement, he also offered condolences for the suffering of Australians during World War Two - singling out the <a href="/wiki/Kokoda_Track_campaign" title="Kokoda Track campaign">Kokoda Track campaign</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sandakan_Death_Marches" title="Sandakan Death Marches">Sandakan Death Marches</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> He was the first Japanese PM to address the Australian parliament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Macri_%26_Abe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2996" data-file-width="2389" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Macri_%26_Abe.jpg/220px-Macri_%26_Abe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Macri_%26_Abe.jpg/330px-Macri_%26_Abe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Macri_%26_Abe.jpg/440px-Macri_%26_Abe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Macri_%26_Abe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Abe with Argentine President, <a href="/wiki/Mauricio_Macri" title="Mauricio Macri">Mauricio Macri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">Buenos Aires</a>, November 2016.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In January 2014, Abe became the first Japanese leader to attend India's <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Republic_Day_parade" title="Delhi Republic Day parade">Republic Day Parade</a> in Delhi as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_state_visits_to_India#Republic_Day_Parade_Guests" title="List of state visits to India">chief guest</a>, during a three-day visit where he and Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Manmohan_Singh" title="Manmohan Singh">Manmohan Singh</a> agreed to increase co-operation over economic, defence and security issues and signed trade agreements related to energy, tourism and telecoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> A close relationship was anticipated between Abe and Narendra Modi after the latter's election as <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_India" title="Prime Minister of India">Prime Minister of India</a> in May 2014, when it was noted that they had established ties from at least seven years previously when Modi was still <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chief_Minister_of_Gujarat" title="Chief Minister of Gujarat">Chief Minister of Gujarat</a> and that Modi was one of three people Abe "followed" on <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a>. The two men exchanged congratulatory messages after the election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Modi made his first major foreign visit to Japan in autumn of 2014, where he and Abe discussed agreements on nuclear co-operation, <a href="/wiki/Rare_earth_element" title="Rare earth element">rare earth elements</a> and joint maritime exercises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> During the visit Abe invited Modi to become the first Indian leader to stay at the Imperial State Guest House in <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 30 May 2014, Abe told officials from the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Southeast_Asian_Nations" title="Association of Southeast Asian Nations">ASEAN</a> countries, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, that Japan wanted to play a major role in maintaining regional security, a departure from the passiveness it has displayed since World War II. He offered Japan's support to other countries in resolving territorial disputes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JapanAsia_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-JapanAsia-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>Relations between Japan and its immediate neighbours, China and South Korea, remained poor after Abe's return to office. While he declared that the "doors are always open on my side", no bilateral meetings between Abe and the Chinese leadership took place for the first 23 months of his second term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Japan_Times_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Japan_Times-107">[107]</a></sup> Neither did Abe hold any meetings with President <a href="/wiki/Park_Geun-hye" title="Park Geun-hye">Park Geun-hye</a> of Korea during his 2012-14 term of office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Guardian_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Guardian-108">[108]</a></sup> Both countries criticised Abe's <a href="/wiki/Controversies_surrounding_Yasukuni_Shrine#Shinzo_Abe" title="Controversies surrounding Yasukuni Shrine">visit to Yasukuni Shrine</a> in December 2013, with China's Foreign Minister describing the action as moving Japan in an "extremely dangerous" direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> In addition, China has continued to criticise Abe's defense reform policies, warning that Japan should not abandon its post-war policy of pacifism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Abe's speech at the World Economic Forum in 2014 was interpreted as a criticism of Chinese foreign and defense policy when he said that "the dividends of growth in Asia must not be wasted on military expansion" and called for greater preservation of the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_seas" title="Freedom of the seas">freedom of the seas</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_law" title="Rule of law">rule of law</a>, although he did not specifically refer to any one country during his remarks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>In November 2014, Abe met China's President <a href="/wiki/Xi_Jinping" title="Xi Jinping">Xi Jinping</a> at the <a href="/wiki/APEC_China_2014" title="APEC China 2014">APEC meeting in Beijing</a> for the first time since either had taken office, after a photocall that was described as "awkward" by the press. Abe later told reporters that during the meeting he suggested establishing a hotline between Tokyo and Beijing to help resolve any maritime clashes, and that the "first step" had been taken to improve relations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Japan_Times_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Japan_Times-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defense_and_security_policy">Defense and security policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Defense and security policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
