<p>Abe has attempted to centralize security policy in the Prime Minister's office by creating the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Council_(Japan)" title="National Security Council (Japan)">Japanese National Security Council</a> to better coordinate national security policy, and by ordering the first National Security Strategy in Japan's history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Based on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Security_Council" title="United States National Security Council">American body of the same name</a>, the law to create the NSC was passed in November 2013 and began operating the following month when Abe appointed <a href="/wiki/Shotaro_Yachi" title="Shotaro Yachi">Shotaro Yachi</a> as Japan's first National Security Advisor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 2013, Abe announced a five-year plan of military expansion. He described this as "proactive pacificism", with the goal of making Japan a more "normal" country, able to defend itself. This was in reaction to a Chinese buildup and a decreased American influence in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the same month the Diet passed the Abe cabinet's <a href="/wiki/State_Secrecy_Law" title="State Secrecy Law">State Secrecy Law</a>, which took effect in December 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> The law expanded the scope for the government to designate what information constitutes a state secret and increased penalties for bureaucurats and journalists who leak such information to up to 10 years in prison and a 10 million yen fine. The passage of the law proved controversial, with thousands protesting the bill in Tokyo and the cabinet's approval rating falling below 50 percent for the first time in some polls. Detractors argued that the law was ambiguous and therefore gave the government too much freedom to decide which information to classify, that it could curtail freedom of the press, and that the cabinet had rushed the legislation without including any corresponding freedom of information guarantees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Abe argued that the law was necessary and applied only in cases of national security, diplomacy, public safety and counter-terrorism, saying, "If the law prevents films from being made, or weakens freedom of the press, I'll resign".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> However he did concede that, in retrospect, the government should have explained the details of the bill more carefully to the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2014 the Abe cabinet took the decision to re-interpret <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Japan" title="Constitution of Japan">Japan's constitution</a> to allow for the right of "Collective Self-Defense". This would allow the <a href="/wiki/Japan_Self-Defense_Forces" title="Japan Self-Defense Forces">Self Defense Forces</a> to come to the aid of, and defend, an ally under attack, whereas the previous interpretation of the constitution was strictly pacifist and allowed for force to be used only in absolute self-defense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> The decision was supported by the United States, which has argued for greater scope for action by Japan as a regional ally, and led to a revision of the U.S.-Japan defense cooperation guidelines in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> In response the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Foreign_Affairs_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Ministry of Foreign Affairs of the People's Republic of China">Chinese Foreign Ministry</a> said the decision "raised doubts" about Japan's commitment to peace, and argued that the Japanese public is opposed to the concept of collective self-defense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Abe argued that the move would not lead to Japan becoming involved in "foreign wars" such as the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iraq_war" title="Iraq war">Iraq war</a>, but instead would secure peace through deterrence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> This led to the introduction of the 2015 security legislation to give legal effect to the cabinet's decision (see below).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2014_cabinet_reshuffle">2014 cabinet reshuffle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: 2014 cabinet reshuffle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The cabinet inaugurated in December 2012 was the longest serving and most stable in post-war Japanese history, lasting 617 days without a change in personnel until Abe conducted a reshuffle in September 2014, with the stated aim of promoting more women into ministerial posts. The reshuffled cabinet tied the record of 5 women ministers set by the first Koizumi cabinet. Most key figures, such as Deputy Prime Minister Aso and Chief Cabinet Secretary Suga, were kept in post although Abe moved Justice Minister Sadakazu Tanigaki out of cabinet to become Secretary General of the LDP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> However, on October 20 two of the women promoted in the reshuffe, Justice Minister <a href="/wiki/Midori_Matsushima" title="Midori Matsushima">Midori Matsushima</a> and Trade Minister <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuko_Obuchi" title="Yuko Obuchi">Yuko Obuchi</a>, were forced to resign in separate election finance scandals. Abe told the press "As prime minister, I bear full responsibility for having appointed them, and I apologize deeply to the people of Japan."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2014_general_election">2014 general election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: 2014 general election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Japanese_Prime_Minister_Abe_Shinzo_-_Akihabara_-_Dec_13_2014.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
(video) Prime Minister Abe giving a speech in front of the Gundam Cafe in <a href="/wiki/Akihabara" title="Akihabara">Akihabara</a>, 2014.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In November 2014, while Abe was attending the APEC forum meeting in China and the G20 Summit in Australia, rumours began appearing in the press that he was planning to call a snap election in the event that he decided to delay the second stage of the consumption tax increase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> It was speculated that Abe planned to do this to "reset" Diet business after it had become gridlocked due to the fallout from ministerial resignations in October, or because the political situation would be less favourable to re-election in 2015 and 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 17 November GDP figures were released that showed Japan had fallen into recession, the two quarters of negative growth following the first stage in the consumption tax rise in April.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Abe held a press conference on 21 November and announced that he was delaying the rise in the consumption tax by 18 months, from October 2015 to April 2017, and calling a snap general election for 14 December. Abe described the election as the "Abenomics Dissolution" and asked the voters to pass judgement on his economic policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Abe's popularity fell slightly with the announcement and he declared that he would resign if his coalition did not win a simple majority, though analysts agreed this was highly unlikely due to the weak state of the opposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> The opposition parties attempted to field a united front in opposition to Abe's policies, but found themselves divided on them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the elections, the LDP won 291 seats, a loss of 3, but the Komeito gained 4 to win 35. Therefore, the governing coalition maintained its two thirds majority in a slightly-reduced lower house of 475.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_term_as_Prime_Minister_.282014.E2.80.93present.29">Third term as Prime Minister (2014–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Third term as Prime Minister (2014–present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abe_Cabinet_Approval_Ratings.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="292" data-file-width="650" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Abe_Cabinet_Approval_Ratings.svg/250px-Abe_Cabinet_Approval_Ratings.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Abe_Cabinet_Approval_Ratings.svg/375px-Abe_Cabinet_Approval_Ratings.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Abe_Cabinet_Approval_Ratings.svg/500px-Abe_Cabinet_Approval_Ratings.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abe_Cabinet_Approval_Ratings.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Abe Cabinet Approval Ratings since 2012</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On December 24 Abe was re-elected to the position of Prime Minister by the House of Representatives, the only change he made when introducing his third cabinet was replacing defense minister <a href="/wiki/Akinori_Eto" title="Akinori Eto">Akinori Eto</a>, who was also involved in a political funding controversy, with <a href="/wiki/Gen_Nakatani" title="Gen Nakatani">Gen Nakatani</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> In his February policy speech, Abe called upon the new Diet to enact “most drastic reforms since the end of World War II” in the areas of the economy, agriculture, healthcare and other sectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy_3">Foreign policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Foreign policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Obama_%26_Abe_Review_Troops_2015.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="436" data-file-width="654" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Obama_%26_Abe_Review_Troops_2015.jpg/250px-Obama_%26_Abe_Review_Troops_2015.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Obama_%26_Abe_Review_Troops_2015.jpg/375px-Obama_%26_Abe_Review_Troops_2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Obama_%26_Abe_Review_Troops_2015.jpg/500px-Obama_%26_Abe_Review_Troops_2015.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Obama_%26_Abe_Review_Troops_2015.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Abe and Former US President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> review troops during the former’s visit to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_DC" title="Washington DC">Washington DC</a> in April 2015</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On a tour of the Middle East in January 2015, Abe announced that Japan would provide 200 million dollars in non-military assistance to countries fighting against the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant</a> as part of a 2.5 billion dollar aid package.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Shortly after this, ISIL released a video in which a masked figure (identified as Mohammed Emwazi or "<a href="/wiki/Jihadi_John" title="Jihadi John">Jihadi John</a>") threatened to kill two Japanese hostages, <a href="/wiki/Kenji_Goto" title="Kenji Goto">Kenji Goto</a> and Haruna Yukawa, in retaliation for the move unless Abe's government paid 200 million dollars of ransom money. Abe cut short his trip to deal with the crisis, declared that such acts of terrorism were "unforgivable" and promised to save the hostages, while refusing to pay the ransom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> The Abe cabinet worked with the Jordanian government to attempt to secure the release of both hostages, after further videos were released by ISIL linking their fate to that of pilot <a href="/wiki/Muath_Al-Kasasbeh" title="Muath Al-Kasasbeh">Muath Al-Kasasbeh</a>, with deputy foreign minister <a href="/wiki/Yasuhide_Nakayama" title="Yasuhide Nakayama">Yasuhide Nakayama</a> conducting negotiations in <a href="/wiki/Amman" title="Amman">Amman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Both hostages were killed, ISIL releasing news of Yukawa's death on January 24 and Goto's on January 31. Abe condemned the killings as a "heinous act", declared that Japan would "not give in to terrorism" and pledged to work with the international community to bring the killers to justice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> There was some criticism of Abe for his move to pledge aid against ISIL while they were holding Japanese citizens hostage, but polls showed support for his administration increasing in the aftermath of the crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> He later used the example of the hostage crisis to argue the case for the collective self-defense legislation that his government introduced in the summer of 2015 (see below).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:181px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Japan-China_Summit_Meeting_at_Jakarta_on_April_22,_2015.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="230" data-file-width="179" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a7/Japan-China_Summit_Meeting_at_Jakarta_on_April_22%2C_2015.jpg" width="179"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Japan-China_Summit_Meeting_at_Jakarta_on_April_22,_2015.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Abe and Chinese President <a href="/wiki/Xi_Jinping" title="Xi Jinping">Xi Jinping</a> meet in Jakarta, April 2015</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In April 2015, he addressed a joint sitting of the U.S. Congress, the first Japanese prime minister to do so. In his speech he referred to the Japan-US Alliance as the "Alliance of Hope", promised that Japan would play a more active security and defence role in the alliance and argued that the TPP would bring both economic and security benefits to the Asia-Pacific region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mofa.go.jp_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-mofa.go.jp-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> The address served as part of a state visit to the United States, the eighth of the Obama Presidency, which the president referred to as a "celebration of the ties of friendship" between America and Japan. During the visit, Abe attended a state dinner at the White House.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>Like his predecessors <a href="/wiki/Tomiichi_Murayama" title="Tomiichi Murayama">Tomiichi Murayama</a> and Junichiro Koizumi, Abe issued a statement commemorating the 70th anniversary of the end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> on August 14, 2015. This statement had been widely anticipated, with some commentators expecting Abe to amend or even refuse to repeat the previous leaders' apologies for Japan's role in the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> In the statement, Abe committed to uphold the previous apologies and expressed "profound grief and eternal, sincere condolences" for the "immeasurable damage and suffering" Japan had caused for "innocent people" during the conflict. He also argued that Japan should not be "predestined to apologise" forever, noting that more than eighty percent of Japanese people alive today were born after the conflict and played no part in it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> The governments of both China and South Korea responded with criticism of the statement, but analysts noted that it was muted and restrained in tone, in comparison to the harsher rhetoric that has been used previously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> A representative of the US National Security Council welcomed the statement, and referred to Japan as having been a "model for nations everywhere" in its record on "peace, democracy, and the rule of law" since the war's end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> Professor Gerald Curtis of Colombia University argued that the statement "probably satisfies no constituency" either in Japan or abroad, but that by repeating the words "aggression", "colonialism", "apology" and "remorse" used in the <a href="/wiki/Murayama_Statement" title="Murayama Statement">Murayama Statement</a> of 1995, it was likely to be enough to improve relations with China and Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Rodrigo_Duterte_3_9.6.16.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="510" data-file-width="765" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Rodrigo_Duterte_3_9.6.16.jpg/220px-Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Rodrigo_Duterte_3_9.6.16.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Rodrigo_Duterte_3_9.6.16.jpg/330px-Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Rodrigo_Duterte_3_9.6.16.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Rodrigo_Duterte_3_9.6.16.jpg/440px-Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Rodrigo_Duterte_3_9.6.16.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Rodrigo_Duterte_3_9.6.16.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Abe meeting with President <a href="/wiki/Rodrigo_Duterte" title="Rodrigo Duterte">Rodrigo Duterte</a> of the Philippines</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In December 2015, Abe and Indian Prime Minister Narendra Modi signed deals in which India agreed to buy <i><a href="/wiki/Shinkansen" title="Shinkansen">Shinkansen</a></i> technology from Japan, (financed in part by a loan from the Japanese government), and for Japan to be raised to full partner status in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exercise_Malabar" title="Exercise Malabar">Malabar naval exercises</a>. Also agreed at the talks was a proposal for Japan to sell non-military nuclear technology to India, to be formally signed once technical details were finalised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> Demonstrating their close relationship, Abe described Modi's policies as "like <i>Shinkansen</i> - high speed, safe and reliable while carrying many people along". In return, Modi complimented Abe as a "phenomenal leader", noted how India-Japan relations had a "wonderful human touch" and invited him to attend the <i>Ganga aarti</i> ceremony at <a href="/wiki/Dashashwamedh_Ghat" title="Dashashwamedh Ghat">Dashashwamedh Ghat</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Varanasi_(Lok_Sabha_constituency)" title="Varanasi (Lok Sabha constituency)">Varanasi</a> constituency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Analysts described the nuclear deal as part of Japan and India's efforts to respond to growing Chinese power in the Asia-Pacific region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Seoul in November 2015, Abe attended the first <a href="/wiki/China%E2%80%93Japan%E2%80%93South_Korea_trilateral_summit" title="China–Japan–South Korea trilateral summit">China–Japan–South Korea trilateral summit</a> held for three years with Korean President Park Geun-hye and Chinese Premier <a href="/wiki/Li_Keqiang" title="Li Keqiang">Li Keqiang</a>. The summits had been suspended in 2012 due to tensions over historical and territorial issues. The leaders agreed to restore the summits as annual events, negotiate a trilateral free trade agreement and work to check <a href="/wiki/North_Korea_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="North Korea and weapons of mass destruction">North Korea's nuclear weapons programme</a>, and announced that trilateral co-operation had been "completely restored".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Donald_Trump_(3).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="334" data-file-width="496" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Donald_Trump_%283%29.jpg/220px-Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Donald_Trump_%283%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Donald_Trump_%283%29.jpg/330px-Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Donald_Trump_%283%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Donald_Trump_%283%29.jpg/440px-Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Donald_Trump_%283%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Donald_Trump_(3).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Abe and the current US President <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>, November 2016</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Japan's relations with <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> have improved somewhat during Abe's third term, in the aftermath of Abe's war anniversary statement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Abe and Korea's President <a href="/wiki/Park_Geun-hye" title="Park Geun-hye">Park Geun-hye</a> held their first bilateral meeting in November 2015, where they both agreed to resolve the issue of so-called "<a href="/wiki/Comfort_women" title="Comfort women">Comfort women</a>" which Park described as the biggest obstacle to closer ties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Guardian_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Guardian-108">[108]</a></sup> In late December 2015, foreign ministers Fumio Kishida and <a href="/wiki/Yun_Byung-se" title="Yun Byung-se">Yun Byung-se</a> announced in Seoul that a deal had been reached to resolve the "comfort women" issue, in which Japan agreed to pay 1 billion yen into a fund to support the 46 surviving victims, and issued a statement that contained Abe's "most sincere apologies and remorse". Abe later telephoned Park to issue the apology. In return, the South Korean government agreed to consider the matter "finally and irreversibly resolved" and work to remove a statue from in front of the Japanese embassy in Seoul. Both sides agreed to refrain from criticising each other over the issue in the future. President Park stated that the agreement would be a "new starting point" for relations between the two countries, although both leaders received some domestic criticism: Abe for issuing the apology, Park for accepting the deal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Security_and_defense_issues">Security and defense issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Security and defense issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In his April speech to Congress, Abe announced that his government would "enact all necessary bills by this coming summer" to expand the <a href="/wiki/Japan_Self-Defense_Forces" title="Japan Self-Defense Forces">Self-Defense Forces</a>' capacity for operations and to give effect to the cabinet's July 2014 decision to re-interpret the constitution in favour of collective self-defense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mofa.go.jp_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-mofa.go.jp-144">[144]</a></sup> Therefore, the Abe cabinet introduced 11 bills making up the <a href="/wiki/2015_Japanese_military_legislation" title="2015 Japanese military legislation">"Peace and Security Preservation Legislation"</a> into the Diet in May 2015, which pushed for a limited expansion of military powers to fight in foreign conflict. The principle aims of the bills were to allow Japan's Self-Defense Forces to come to the aid of allied nations under attack (even if Japan itself was not), to expand their scope to support international peacekeeping operations, and to allow for Japan to take on a greater share of security responsibilities as part of the <a href="/wiki/Japan%E2%80%93United_States_relations" title="Japan–United States relations">US-Japan Alliance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reference_to_NY_times_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-reference_to_NY_times-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Diplomat_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Diplomat-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Vladimir_Putin_(2016-11-20)_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1160" data-file-width="1880" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Vladimir_Putin_%282016-11-20%29_01.jpg/220px-Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Vladimir_Putin_%282016-11-20%29_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Vladimir_Putin_%282016-11-20%29_01.jpg/330px-Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Vladimir_Putin_%282016-11-20%29_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Vladimir_Putin_%282016-11-20%29_01.jpg/440px-Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Vladimir_Putin_%282016-11-20%29_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shinz%C5%8D_Abe_and_Vladimir_Putin_(2016-11-20)_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Abe and Russian President <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Putin" title="Vladimir Putin">Vladimir Putin</a>. Japan have not yet signed a peace treaty with Russia to end <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> because of a <a href="/wiki/Kuril_Islands_dispute" title="Kuril Islands dispute">Kuril Islands dispute</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In order to allow for enough time to pass the bills in the face of lengthy opposition scrutiny, the Abe cabinet extended the Diet session by 95 days from June into September, making it the longest in the post-war era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> The bills passed the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Representatives_(Japan)" title="House of Representatives (Japan)">House of Representatives</a> on July 16 with the support of the majority LDP-Komeito coalition. Diet members from opposition Democratic, <a href="/wiki/Japan_Innovation_Party" title="Japan Innovation Party">Innovation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Communist_Party" title="Japanese Communist Party">Communist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_(Japan)" title="Social Democratic Party (Japan)">Social Democratic</a> parties walked out of the vote in protest at what they said was the government's move to force the bills through without sufficient debate and ignore "responsible opposition parties".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> Abe countered by arguing that the bills had been debated for "as many as 113 hours" before the vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> While common practice in many other <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_system" title="Parliamentary system">parliamentary democracies</a>, a government using its majority to "railroad" controversial bills though the Diet in the face of political and public opposition is a subject of criticism in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a result of these moves, Abe faced a public backlash, and opinion polls showed that his approval ratings fell into negative figures for the first time since he returned to power in 2012, with 50 percent disapproving and 38 percent approving of the cabinet according to one Nikkei survey at the beginning of August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> Many protested the legislation outside the Diet buildings, denouncing what were referred to as "war bills" by opponents. Organisers of the protests estimated that up to 100,000 protesters marched against the bills' passage of the lower house in July.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> During Diet committee hearings on the bills, constitutional scholars (some of whom had been invited by the ruling parties) and a former supreme court justice argued that the legislation was unconstitutional.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Abe was publicly criticised by atomic bomb survivor <a href="/wiki/Sumiteru_Taniguchi" title="Sumiteru Taniguchi">Sumiteru Taniguchi</a> in his speech at the <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki_Peace_Park#Peace_Memorial_Ceremony" title="Nagasaki Peace Park">Nagasaki memorial ceremony</a> on August 9, when he stated that the defense reforms would take Japan "back to the wartime period".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> Members of the Abe cabinet said that they would make a greater effort to explain the contents of and the reasons for the security legislation to the public, with the LDP releasing an animated cartoon commercial, and Abe appearing live on television and internet chat streams to make the case for the legislation and take questions from members of the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>The security bills were finally approved 148 votes to 90 by the House of Councillors and became law on September 19, following opposition attempts at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dilatory_motions_and_tactics" title="Dilatory motions and tactics">delaying tactics</a> and physical altercations in which some Diet members attempted to stop the relevant chairman calling the vote to move the bill out of committee and to a general vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> After the vote, Abe issued a statement saying that the new laws "will fortify our pledge to never again wage war", and that the legislation, rather than being "war bills", was instead "aimed at deterring war and contributing to peace and security". He also pledged to continue to explain the legislation to try to gain "greater understanding" from the public on the issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-179">[179]</a></sup> Following the bills' passage, Abe was expected to once again return his focus to economic issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Diplomat_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Diplomat-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>On October 18, 2015 Abe presided over the triennial fleet review of the <a href="/wiki/Japan_Maritime_Self-Defense_Force" title="Japan Maritime Self-Defense Force">Japan Maritime Self-Defense Force</a> in his role as Commander-in-Chief of the Self-Defense Forces. In his speech to personnel on board the destroyer <i>Kurama</i> he announced that “by highly hoisting the flag of ‘proactive pacifism,’ I’m determined to contribute more than ever to world peace and prosperity”. Later that day he went aboard the <i><a href="/wiki/USS_Ronald_Reagan" title="USS Ronald Reagan">USS Ronald Reagan</a></i>, becoming the first Japanese Prime Minister to set foot on an American warship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 2015 the Abe government announced the creation of a new intelligence unit to aid counter-terrorism operations, to be based in the Foreign Ministry but led by the Prime Minister's Office. This was reported as being part of efforts to step up security measures in preparation for the <a href="/wiki/42nd_G7_summit" title="42nd G7 summit">2016 G7 Summit</a> in <a href="/wiki/Shima,_Mie" title="Shima, Mie">Shima, Mie</a> and <a href="/wiki/2020_Summer_Olympics" title="2020 Summer Olympics">2020 Olympics in Tokyo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> In the same month the cabinet approved Japan's largest ever defense budget, at 5.1 trillion yen, for the fiscal year beginning in April 2016. The package included funding intended for the purchase of three <a href="/wiki/Northrop_Grumman_RQ-4_Global_Hawk" title="Northrop Grumman RQ-4 Global Hawk">"Global Hawk" drones</a>, six <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin_F-35_Lightning_II" title="Lockheed Martin F-35 Lightning II">F-35 fighter jets</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Boeing_KC-46_Pegasus" title="Boeing KC-46 Pegasus">Boeing KC-46A midair refueling aircraft</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Re-election_as_LDP_President_and_.22Abenomics_2.0.22">Re-election as LDP President and "Abenomics 2.0"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title='Edit section: Re-election as LDP President and "Abenomics 2.0"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In September 2015 Abe was re-elected as president of the LDP in an uncontested election after LDP Diet member <a href="/wiki/Seiko_Noda" title="Seiko Noda">Seiko Noda</a> failed to garner enough support to stand as a candidate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> Following this Abe carried out a cabinet reshuffle, once again keeping the key ministers of <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Finance_(Japan)" title="Minister of Finance (Japan)">Finance</a>, Economy, <a href="/wiki/Minister_for_Foreign_Affairs_(Japan)" title="Minister for Foreign Affairs (Japan)">Foreign Affairs</a> and the Chief Cabinet Secretary in post. He also created a new ministerial position for the co-ordination of policies related to the economy, population decline, and social security reform, which was filled by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Katsunobu_Kato" title="Katsunobu Kato">Katsunobu Kato</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup></p>
<p>At a press conference after his official re-election as LDP President, Abe announced that the next stage of his administration would focus on what he called "Abenomics 2.0", the aim of which was to tackle issues of <a href="/wiki/Demography_of_Japan" title="Demography of Japan">low fertility and an aging population</a> and create a society “in which each and every one of Japan’s 100 million citizens can take on active roles”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-179">[179]</a></sup> This new policy consisted of targets which Abe referred to as "three new arrows"; to boost Japan's GDP to 600 trillion yen by 2021, to raise the national fertility rate from an average of 1.4 to 1.8 children per woman and stabilise the population at 100 million, and to create a situation where people would not have to leave employment in order to care for elderly relatives by the mid 2020s. Abe explained that the government would take measures to increase wages, boost consumption, and expand childcare, social security and care services for the elderly to meet these goals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup></p>
<p>This new iteration of Abenomics was met with some criticism by commentators, who argued that it was not yet clear if the first three arrows had succeeded in lifting Japan out of deflation (inflation was some way below the 2 percent target), that the new arrows were merely presented as targets without the necessary policies to meet them, and that the targets themselves were unrealistic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> However, opinion polls during the final months of 2015 showed the Abe cabinet's approval ratings once again climbing into positive figures after the change in emphasis back to economic issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the conclusion of the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Pacific_Partnership" title="Trans-Pacific Partnership">Trans-Pacific Partnership</a> talks in early October 2015, Abe hailed the agreement for creating an “unprecedented economic zone" and opening up possibilities for an even wider Asia-Pacific free trade deal and Japanese trade with Europe. He also promised to mitigate any negative effects on the Japanese agricultural sector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> GDP figures released in November 2015 initially appeared to show that Japan had entered a second recession since the implementation of Abenomics,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> however these figures were subsequently revised to show that the economy had grown by 1 percent in the third quarter, thus avoiding recession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 2015 the two parties making up Abe's governing coalition agreed to introduce a reduced rate of consumption tax for food when the anticipated tax increase from 8 to 10 percent takes place in April 2017. This deal was reached after Abe was seen to come down strongly in favour of the position held by his junior coalition partner the Komeito, that the tax rate should be reduced, which prompted some disagreement from members of his own party, who favoured a policy of greater fiscal consolidation through taxes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> Abe dismissed the chairman of the LDP's tax panel <a href="/wiki/Takeshi_Noda" title="Takeshi Noda">Takeshi Noda</a> (who opposed the reduction), and appointed <a href="/wiki/Yoichi_Miyazawa" title="Yoichi Miyazawa">Yoichi Miyazawa</a>, who was more favourable to the policy, as his replacement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> Abe declared the tax deal to be "the best possible result" of the negotiations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_positions_and_philosophy">Political positions and philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Political positions and philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Affiliated to the openly revisionist organization <a href="/wiki/Nippon_Kaigi" title="Nippon Kaigi">Nippon Kaigi</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> Shinzo Abe consistently defends its agenda. Ms.<a href="/wiki/Tomomi_Inada" title="Tomomi Inada">Tomomi Inada</a> is nominated as next Secretary-General of LDP, by Shinzō Abe and it means that she will be nominated as the successor of Shinzō Abe by him, according to the press in Japan, due to her political and historical beliefs which are close to his.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Views_on_history">Views on history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Views on history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shinzo_Abe_with_KSG_Students.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1467" data-file-width="2431" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Shinzo_Abe_with_KSG_Students.jpg/220px-Shinzo_Abe_with_KSG_Students.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Shinzo_Abe_with_KSG_Students.jpg/330px-Shinzo_Abe_with_KSG_Students.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Shinzo_Abe_with_KSG_Students.jpg/440px-Shinzo_Abe_with_KSG_Students.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shinzo_Abe_with_KSG_Students.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Abe, as the Japanese Chief Cabinet Secretary, with a group of students from <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>. His future Chief Cabinet Secretary <a href="/wiki/Yasuhisa_Shiozaki" title="Yasuhisa Shiozaki">Yasuhisa Shiozaki</a> (himself a graduate of Harvard University) is standing to his left.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Abe is widely viewed as a right-wing nationalist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lucy_Alexander_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lucy_Alexander-201">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_New_York_Times_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_New_York_Times-202">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Justin_McCurry_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-Justin_McCurry-203">[203]</a></sup> The British journalist <a href="/wiki/Rupert_Wingfield-Hayes" title="Rupert Wingfield-Hayes">Rupert Wingfield-Hayes</a> of <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> described him as "far more right wing than most of his predecessors."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rupert_Wingfield-Hayes_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rupert_Wingfield-Hayes-204">[204]</a></sup> Since 1997, as the bureau chief of the 'Institute of Junior Assembly Members Who Think About the Outlook of Japan and History Education', Abe led the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Society_for_History_Textbook_Reform" title="Japanese Society for History Textbook Reform">Japanese Society for History Textbook Reform</a>. On his official homepage<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> he questions the extent to which coercion was applied toward the <a href="/wiki/Comfort_women" title="Comfort women">comfort women</a>, dismissing South Korean positions on the issue, as foreign interference in Japanese domestic affairs. In a Diet session on October 6, 2006, Abe revised his statement regarding comfort women, and said that he accepted the report issued in 1993 by the sitting cabinet secretary, <a href="/wiki/Y%C5%8Dhei_K%C5%8Dno" title="Yōhei Kōno">Yōhei Kōno</a>, where the Japanese government officially acknowledged the issue. Later in the session, Abe stated his belief that Class A war criminals are not criminals under Japan's domestic law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a meeting of the Lower House Budget Committee in February 2006, Shinzō Abe said, 'There is a problem as to how to define aggressive wars; we cannot say it is decided academically',<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20060216minutes_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-20060216minutes-207">[207]</a></sup> and 'It is not the business of the government to decide how to define the last world war. I think we have to wait for the estimation of historians'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20060216minutes_207-1"><a href="#cite_note-20060216minutes-207">[207]</a></sup> However, on a TV program in July 2006<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> he denied that <a href="/wiki/Manchukuo" title="Manchukuo">Manchukuo</a> was a <a href="/wiki/Puppet_state" title="Puppet state">puppet state</a>.</p>
<p>Abe published a book called <i>Toward a Beautiful Nation</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">美しい国へ</span><span class="t_nihongo_comma" style="display:none">,</span> <i>Utsukushii kuni e</i><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>)</span> in July 2006, which became a bestseller in Japan. The Korean and Chinese governments, as well as noted academics and commentators, have voiced concern about Abe's historical views.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 2007, in response to a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> resolution by <a href="/wiki/Mike_Honda" title="Mike Honda">Mike Honda</a>, Abe denied any government coercion in the recruitment of comfort women during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> in line with a statement made almost ten years before on the same issue, in which Abe voiced his opposition to the inclusion of the subject of military prostitution in several school textbooks and then denied any coercion in the "narrow" sense of the word, environmental factors notwithstanding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> This statement provoked negative reactions in Asian and <a href="/wiki/Western_Hemisphere" title="Western Hemisphere">western</a> countries; a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Times" title="New York Times">New York Times</a></i> editorial on March 6, 2007 commented for instance:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>What part of 'Japanese Army sex slaves' does Japan's prime minister, Shinzo Abe, have so much trouble understanding and apologizing for? ... These were not commercial brothels. Force, explicit and implicit, was used in recruiting these women. What went on in them was serial rape, not prostitution. The Japanese Army's involvement is documented in the government's own defense files. A senior Tokyo official more or less apologized for this horrific crime in 1993...Yesterday, [Abe] grudgingly acknowledged the 1993 quasi-apology, but only as part of a pre-emptive declaration that his government would reject the call, now pending in the United States Congress, for an official apology. America isn't the only country interested in seeing Japan belatedly accept full responsibility. [South] Korea and China are also infuriated by years of Japanese equivocations over the issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>A <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_Post" title="Washington Post">Washington Post</a></i> editorial, "Shinzo Abe's Double Talk" (March 24, 2007), also criticized him: "he's passionate about Japanese victims of North Korea—and blind to Japan's own war crimes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> A March 2, 2014 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Times" title="New York Times">New York Times</a> editorial called Abe a "nationalist" who is a profound threat to American-Japanese relations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> and a November 14, 2014 opinion piece labeled Abe's position on the subject of comfort women a "war on truth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup> The same editorial presented him as a <a href="/wiki/Historical_negationism" title="Historical negationism">revisionist</a>, a view largely accepted by the international and part of the Japanese press.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Response_to_mass_media">Response to mass media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Response to mass media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shinzo_Abe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2184" data-file-width="1847" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Shinzo_Abe.jpg/220px-Shinzo_Abe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Shinzo_Abe.jpg/330px-Shinzo_Abe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Shinzo_Abe.jpg/440px-Shinzo_Abe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shinzo_Abe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Abe campaigning in 2010</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Asahi_Shimbun" title="Asahi Shimbun">Asahi Shimbun</a></i> also accused Abe and <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dichi_Nakagawa" title="Shōichi Nakagawa">Shōichi Nakagawa</a> of censoring a 2001 NHK program concerning "The Women's International War Crimes Tribunal".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> The "tribunal" was a private committee to adjudicate comfort women; about 5,000 people including 64 victims from Japan and abroad attended. The committee members, who claimed to be specialists of <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international law</a>, claimed that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Hirohito" title="Emperor Hirohito">Emperor Hirohito</a> and the Japanese government were responsible for the use of comfort women. The TV program, however, did not mention the full name of the tribunal and keywords such as 'Japanese troops' or 'sexual slavery', and it also cut the sight of the tribunal, the host grouping, statements of the organizer, and the judgement itself. Instead, it presented criticism against the tribunal by a right-wing academic and his statement that 'there was no abduction of sex slaves and they were prostitutes'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the day following the <a href="/wiki/Asahi_Shimbun" title="Asahi Shimbun">Asahi Shimbun</a> report, Akira Nagai, the chief producer and primary person responsible for the program, held a press conference and ensured the report of the Asahi Shimbun. Abe stated that the content "had to be broadcast from a neutral point of view" and "what I did is not to give political pressure". Abe said "It was a political terrorism by Asahi Shimbun and it was tremendously clear that they had intention to inhume me and Mr. Nakagawa politically, and it is also clear that it was complete fabrication." He also characterized the tribunal as a "mock trial" and raised objection to the presence of <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korean</a> <a href="/wiki/Prosecutor" title="Prosecutor">prosecutors</a> singling them out as agents of North Korean government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> Abe's actions in the NHK incident have been criticized<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (January 2015)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> as being both illegal (violating the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broadcast_Law" title="Broadcast Law">Broadcast Law</a>) and unconstitutional (violating the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Japan" title="Constitution of Japan">Japanese Constitution</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup></p>
<p>A news program aired on <a href="/wiki/TBS_(U.S._TV_channel)" title="TBS (U.S. TV channel)">TBS</a> on July 21, 2006 about a secret biological weapons troop of Imperial Japanese Army called '<a href="/wiki/Unit_731" title="Unit 731">Unit 731</a>', along with a picture panel of Shinzō Abe, who has no relation to the report. Abe said in a press conference, "It is a truly big problem if they want to injure my political life". The <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Internal_Affairs_and_Communications" title="Ministry of Internal Affairs and Communications">Ministry of Internal Affairs and Communications</a> inquired into fact relevance and stated that there had been an omission in editing the TV program fairly, making an administrative direction of exceptional stringent warning based upon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broadcast_Law" title="Broadcast Law">Broadcast Law</a>.</p>
<p>On October 24, 2006, a report emerged that Abe's new administration had called on the NHK to "pay attention" to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Korean_abductions_of_Japanese" title="North Korean abductions of Japanese">North Korean abductees issue</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> Critics, some even within Abe's own LDP party, charged that the government was violating freedom of expression by meddling in the affairs of the public broadcaster.</p>
<p>In December 2006, it was revealed that former Prime-Minister Junichiro Koizumi's government, in which Abe was Chief Cabinet Secretary, had influenced town hall style meetings, during which paid performers would ask government officials favorable questions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup></p>
<p>On November 22, 2012, it was reported that Tokyo Broadcasting System (TBS) early morning TV show "Asazuba" accidentally displayed Abe's photo alongside a news report about an NHK announcer's arrest for a sex offense. Abe's face filled viewers' screens along with the name of NHK announcer Takeshige Morimoto, who anchors NHK's "Ohayo Nippon" program on Saturday and Sunday. Morimoto was arrested for allegedly groping a woman on the train. Abe posted on his public Facebook page "This morning on the TBS show 'Asazuba,' when a newscaster reported on a story regarding the apprehension of a molester, a photo of me was shown. Images of this blunder can now be seen clearly across the Internet, Have the slander campaigns already begun!? If this were merely an accident, it would be proper for the TV station to give me a personal apology, but as yet I haven't heard a single word." The newscaster acknowledged that the incorrect image had been displayed, but merely stated that the photo was "unrelated" and did not refer to the politician by name. Neither Abe nor his office have received any form of apology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Yasukuni_Shrine">Yasukuni Shrine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Yasukuni Shrine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Abe has visited <a href="/wiki/Yasukuni_Shrine" title="Yasukuni Shrine">Yasukuni Shrine</a> on several occasions. While serving as Chief Cabinet Secretary in the government of Junichiro Koizumi, he visited in April 2006, prompting South Korea to describe the trip as "regrettable".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> He visited again on August 15, 2012, the anniversary of the end of World War II,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> and after winning the presidency of the Liberal Democratic Party, he visited on October 17, 2012 in an official capacity as party president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup></p>
<p>He initially refrained from visiting the shrine as a sitting prime minister. He did not visit at all during his first term from September 2006 to September 2007, unlike his predecessor Koizumi, who had visited yearly while in office. Abe's not visiting the shrine prompted a Japanese nationalist named Yoshihiro Tanjo to cut off his own little finger in protest and mail it to the LDP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> While campaigning for the presidency of the LDP in 2012, Abe said that he regretted not visiting the shrine while Prime Minister. He again refrained from visiting the shrine during the first year of his second stint as Prime Minister in consideration for improving relations with China and Korea, whose leaders refused to meet with Abe during this time. He said on December 9, 2013 that "it is natural that we should express our feelings of respect to the war dead who sacrificed their lives for the nation... but it is my thinking that we should avoid making [Yasukuni visits] political and diplomatic issues." In lieu of visiting, Abe sent ritual offerings to the shrine for festivals in April and October 2013, as well as the anniversary of the end of World War II in August 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup></p>
<p>His first visit to the shrine as Prime Minister took place on December 26, 2013, the first anniversary of his second term in office. It was the first visit to the shrine by a sitting prime minister since Junichiro Koizumi visited in August 2006. Abe said that he "prayed to pay respect for the war dead who sacrificed their precious lives and hoped that they rest in peace" The Chinese government published a protest that day, calling government visits to the shrine "an effort to glorify the Japanese militaristic history of external invasion and colonial rule and to challenge the outcome of World War II."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup> Qin Gang of the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Foreign_Affairs_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Ministry of Foreign Affairs of the People's Republic of China">Ministry of Foreign Affairs</a> said: Abe is "unwelcome by Chinese people.. Chinese leaders won't meet him any more."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Mainichi_Shimbun" title="Mainichi Shimbun">Mainichi Shimbun</a></i> argued in an editorial that the visit could also "cast a dark shadow" on relations with the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup> and the US embassy in Tokyo released a statement that "the United States is disappointed that Japan's leadership has taken an action that will exacerbate tensions with Japan's neighbors."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i> reported that U.S. officials urge Abe not to visit the shrine and pay homage to war criminals anymore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup> Public intellectual <a href="/wiki/Amitai_Etzioni" title="Amitai Etzioni">Amitai Etzioni</a> of the Institute for Communitarian Policy Studies, who was a child in Germany when the Nazis rose to power, has stated in response to Abe's visits, "Unlike Japan, [Germany] faced their past, came to terms with it and learned from it. Japan should do the same."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> Etzioni criticized Prime Minister Abe's visit to the shrine as well as what he refers to as Japan's recent "nationalist wave" in an op-ed for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Diplomat" title="The Diplomat">The Diplomat</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> On August 15, 2014, the 69th anniversary of the surrender of Japan in World War II, Abe chose to not visit the shrine, in what was perceived as a diplomatic gesture to South Korea, China and Taiwan. Despite Abe's absence, China and South Korea both voiced their disapproval at Japan's leadership as a large number of politicians, and three cabinet members did attend the shrine to mark the anniversary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AbeAbsence_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-AbeAbsence-242">[242]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Restoration_of_Sovereignty_Day">Restoration of Sovereignty Day</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Restoration of Sovereignty Day">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On April 28, 2013, a new public event, the <a href="/wiki/Restoration_of_Sovereignty_Day" title="Restoration of Sovereignty Day">Restoration of Sovereignty Day</a>, was held in Tokyo to mark the 61st anniversary of the end of the US <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Japan" title="Occupation of Japan">occupation of Japan</a>. It had been proposed by Abe in 2012. The event, which was attended by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Akihito" title="Emperor Akihito">Emperor Akihito</a>, was denounced by many Okinawans who saw it as celebrating a betrayal, and there were demonstrations in both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Okinawa" title="Okinawa">Okinawa</a> and Tokyo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_rsd_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_rsd-243">[243]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immigration">Immigration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Immigration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2015, Abe's government refused to admit <a href="/wiki/Refugee" title="Refugee">refugees</a> affected by conflicts in the Middle East and Africa. Abe stated that Japan must solve its own problems before accepting any immigrants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup> Abe has favored short-term working visas for migrant workers to "work and raise incomes for a limited period of time, and then return home."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Honors.2C_awards_and_international_recognition">Honors, awards and international recognition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Honors, awards and international recognition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cabinets">Cabinets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Cabinets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_term_.282006.E2.80.932007.29">First term (2006–2007)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: First term (2006–2007)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Abe's first cabinet was announced on September 26, 2006. The only minister retained in his position from the previous Koizumi cabinet was Foreign Minister Taro Aso, who had been one of Abe's competitors for the LDP presidency. In addition to the cabinet positions existing under Koizumi, Abe created five new "advisor" positions. He reshuffled his cabinet on August 27, 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup></p>

<td colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Eriko_Yamatani" title="Eriko Yamatani">Eriko Yamatani</a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>Public Relations Advisor</th>
<td colspan="2"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hiroshige_Seko" title="Hiroshige Seko">Hiroshige Seko</a></td>

<p>Notes:</p>
<ol>
<li><a href="/wiki/Toshikatsu_Matsuoka" title="Toshikatsu Matsuoka">Toshikatsu Matsuoka</a> committed suicide on May 28, 2007, hours before being due for questioning in connection to allegations of misappropriation of government funds. He was replaced by Norihiko Akagi, who himself resigned on August 1, 2007 due to suspicions of similar conduct. <a href="/wiki/Masatoshi_Wakabayashi" title="Masatoshi Wakabayashi">Masatoshi Wakabayashi</a> was appointed Agriculture Minister, which he served concurrently with his post as Environment Minister.</li>
<li>Masatoshi Wakabayashi was appointed Agriculture Minister on September 3, 2007, following <a href="/wiki/Takehiko_Endo" title="Takehiko Endo">Takehiko Endo</a>'s resignation due to a financial scandal.</li>
<li>Prior to Abe's administration, this post was known as "Director General of the Defense Agency". In December 2006, its status was elevated to ministry level.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fumio_Kyuma" title="Fumio Kyuma">Fumio Kyuma</a> resigned on July 3, 2007 for controversial remarks made about the atomic bombing of Hiroshima. He was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Yuriko_Koike" title="Yuriko Koike">Yuriko Koike</a>, then National Security Advisor.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yoshimi_Watanabe" title="Yoshimi Watanabe">Yoshimi Watanabe</a> was appointed Minister of State for Administrative Reform upon December 28, 2007 resignation of <a href="/wiki/Genichiro_Sata" title="Genichiro Sata">Genichiro Sata</a>. He served in this capacity concurrently with his role as Minister of State for Regulatory Reform.</li>
</ol>

<p><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family">Family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinz%C5%8D_Abe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Abe's father Shintaro Abe served in the House of Representatives from 1958 to 1991 and was foreign minister from 1982 to 1986; he is the son of Kan Abe who served in the House from 1937 to 1946. Abe's mother Yoko Abe is the daughter of Nobusuke Kishi, a former prime minister who was at one time imprisoned as a "Class A" war crimes suspect following the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-akie_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-akie-258">[258]</a></sup> His older brother, Hironobu Abe, became president and CEO of Mitsubishi Shōji Packaging Corporation, while his younger brother, <a href="/wiki/Nobuo_Kishi" title="Nobuo Kishi">Nobuo Kishi</a>, became Senior Vice-Minister for Foreign Affairs.</p>
<p>Abe married <a href="/wiki/Akie_Abe" title="Akie Abe">Akie Matsuzaki</a>, a socialite and former radio disk jockey, in 1987. She is the daughter of the president of <a href="/wiki/Morinaga" title="Morinaga">Morinaga</a>, a chocolate manufacturer. She is popularly known as the "domestic opposition party" due to her outspoken views, which often contradict her husband's. Following her husband's first stint as prime minister, she opened an organic <a href="/wiki/Izakaya" title="Izakaya">izakaya</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Kanda,_Tokyo" title="Kanda, Tokyo">Kanda</a> district of Tokyo, but is not active in management due to the urging of her mother-in-law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-akie_258-1"><a href="#cite_note-akie-258">[258]</a></sup> The couple have no children, having undergone unsuccessful fertility treatments earlier in their marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup></p>
